On the title:
MMW 'vṛṣa':  "Justice or Virtue personified as a bull or as "Siva's bull Mn. viii, 16 Pur. Kāvyād.; just or virtuous act, virtue, moral merit "Siś. Vās.;"

On the title, see De Simini 2016:238 n. 13: ''As noted by Sanderson [...], this title can have a double meaning, since the ‘bull’ (vṛṣa) is both a synonym of ‘religious practice’ and the traditional mount (vāhana) of Śiva.

'Muta cum liquida' (Balogh 2018?:33 note 6) find sources on metrical licenses / -ces
Apte Appendix A p. 1: pra, bra, hra, kra are exceptions
In the text below, śra, śya, śva, sva, dva seem additional ones (plus tra and vra? and rp? 11.5X)

''According to Kedārabhaṭṭa in Vṛttaratnākara, a short vowel followed by a consonant cluster (i.e., multiple consonants), although guru by default, can optionally be treated as laghu if that consonant cluster happens to be the beginning of a new word. Implement this option. (With required UI changes, e.g. saying: "this verse can be read as <...> if an option is used in places x, y, z.") ''
SOURCE: ( https://github.com/shreevatsa/sanskrit/issues/1 )

Vṛttaratnākara 1.10! (etext downloaded):
padādāv iha varṇasya saṃyogaḥ kramasaṃjñikaḥ /
puraḥsthitena tena syāl laghutā 'pi kvacid guroḥ // KVrk_1.10 //

SOURCE: ( https://github.com/shreevatsa/sanskrit/issues/1 ):
``The traditional rule is that the presence of a conjunct consonant (consonant cluster) makes the preceding syllable a guru syllable. For purists, this is an inviolable rule, and there are no exceptions.

However, under the influence of Prākṛta and deśa-bhāṣā prosody, and also music, later prosodists give poets the option to either conform to this rule, or to occasionally indulge in an exception. This exception is stated differently by different authors:

    In Kannada and Telugu prosody, under the name of śithila-dvitva, the exception is that sometimes in a consonant cluster of the form [consonant + "r"], the "r" (repha) can be ignored, so that it is not a conjunct consonant anymore.
    Kedāra-bhaṭṭa in his Vṛtta-ratnākara states the exception as: if the consonant cluster is at the beginning of a word, then it may be treated as a single consonant.
    Yet another way of stating the exception is that a consonant cluster can be optionally treated as a single consonant if the effort of pronouncing it is quick (तीव्रप्रयत्नेन) or with less effort (श्रममन्तरा).

All ways of stating the exception cover the example of "निद्रव्यो ह्रियमेति ह्रीपरिगतः प्रभ्रश्यते तेजसः", but Kedārabhaṭṭa's way doesn't cover the example of "राज्ञां मध्ये सपदि जह्रिषे मित्रविन्दामवन्तीम्" from the Nārāyaṇīyam. So far I don't see a reason to prefer Kedārabhaṭṭa's formulation over the others.

But anyway, to return to the main point:

    The exception is not accepted by purists: Shatavadhani Ganesh says that the Sanskrit masters like Kālidāsa, Bhāravi, Māgha, Śrīharṣa, and Viśākhadatta have not freely used this exception (though the masters in Kannada and other languages have).
    Being more of a "poetic licence" and a violation of the standard rule (only found in later poetry), it is extremely unlikely that any sane poet would have indulged in that exception in all four pāda-s of a verse.
    Thus it is very unlikely that the program will miss identifying a verse that indulges in this exception. At least, I haven't seen any example from real life so far. (Just saying that because it would be easy to intentionally compose a verse to disproves this!)

Reference: see comments by Dr. Ganesh and Nityananda Misra in this thread started by Vishvas Vasuki:
https://groups.google.com/forum/#!topic/bvparishat/ya1cGLuhc14/discussion
''

Nirajan's advice was to look into Chandomañjarī, and here you are:

atra pādāntago laghur gurur bhaved| yathā| 
taruṇaṃ va?rṣapaśākaṃ navaudanaṃ picchilāni ca dadhīni?|
alpavyayena sundari grāmyajano miṣṭam aśnāti||
sundarīti grāmyaśabde pare [because it is followed by the word grāmya] vikalpena [option] laghutvam|
tathā bhaṭṭiḥ|

lulitapatatrimālaṃ rugṇāśanavāṇakesaratamālam|
.   . .   . -  . - -  -  -    -! -   . - - -
sa vanaṃ viviktamālaṃ sītāṃ draṣṭuṃ jagāmālam|

Umāmaheśvarasaṃvāda (s. no. 652), fols. 218v– 263v; Śivopaniṣad (s. no. 636), fols. 264r–297v; 
Uttarottarama-hāsaṃvāda (s. no.  654), fols. 298r–324r; Vṛṣasārasaṃgraha (s. no. 657), fols. 325r–390r; 
Dharmapu- trikā (s. no. 608), fols. 391r–406r. Described in: Dominik Wujastyk (1985). A Handlist of the 
Sanskrit and Prakrit Manuscripts in the Library of the Wellcome Institute for the History of Medicine, 
vol. 1. London, The Wellcome Institute for the History of Medicine.

De Simini 2016:240 n. 19 Śivadharma MSS:
¤¤¤KALKATTA¤¤¤
17 -- ASC G 4076 Vṛṣasārasaṃgraha (cat. no. 4083)


Nirajan using this MS:
The Manuscript NP 57

It is a multiple-text palm-leaf manuscript written in Newari script and preserved in de la collection Sylvain Lévi à
l’Institut d’études indiennes, collège de France. The manuscript number is: MS. Skt. 57-B. 23. It contains 249
palm leaves, each folio containing six lines. The following palm leaves are missing: 3, 8, 47, 48, 135, 197, 214 et
216. Foliation is in the verso: on middle of the lef-hand margin in combination of Newari letters and in the
middle of right-hand in roman numbers by a second hand. There are two binding holes: one in the centre lef and
one in the centre right. The manuscript is some times damaged in margins with considerable loos of the text. The
text is written in a clear hand and contains few mistakes. Although it is undated manuscript, it could be dated to
the 11th century AD on the palaeographical grounds.3 It contains the following text in the order they are
presented in the manuscript: Śivadharmaśāstra (fols. 1–40), Śivadharmottaraśāstra (fols. 40–93),
Śivadharmasaṃgraha (fols. 94–142), Umāmaheśvarasaṃvāda (fols. 143–172), Śivopaniṣad (fols. 173– 189),
Uttarottaramahāsaṃvāda (fols. 190–211), Vṛṣasārasaṃgraha (fols. 212–252), Dharmaputrikā (fols. 253–262). This
source contains reliable readings and contains few scribble mistakes.Śivāśramā- dhyāya covers fols. 33v4–37r3.

Nirajan says it reads close to Naraharinātha's edition

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
IDEAS:
- is this text really Śaiva? why in this collection?
- niśvāsa as sadāśiva in ch. 16; Niśvāsa uttarasūtra 5.50-51; see also Kafle Niśvāsamukha p.11ff; ibid. p.12: "The term niśvāsa means sighing. Thus, an alternative
meaning of the Niśvāsatattvasaṃhitā could also be a ‘‘sighing tantra.’’ To be more precise,
a tantra that originated from the sighing of Śiva. This is to say, the speech of Śiva."
- tattva-system: mati and suśira (ch. 20)
- parallels: MBh, Bṛhatkālottara, 
- ch. 21: Viṣṇu; is this a Śaiva text?
- āśramas are in an order different from usual; compare this to NĀT; "Variations on the āśrama-system"

History of Dharmasastra 2.1
pp. 416ff on āśramas

n. 988! see Āpastamba-dharma-sūtra ii.9.21.1: catvāra āśramā gārhasthyam ācāryakulaṃ maunaṃ vānaprasthyam iti| Quoted by Śankara
But the chapters in Āpastamba follow the traditional order.
"Āp. places the householder first among the āśramas, probably on account of the importance of that stage to all other āśramas." Kane ibid.

ibid p. 417: person in last āśrama is called: parivrāṭ, parivrājaka(!), bhikṣu, muni, yati.
See 
Olivelle, Patrick. The Āśrama System. The History and Hermeneutics of a Religious Institution. New York, Oxford: Oxford University Press, 1993.  [megvan] p.82ff: The Order of Āśramas; 
        ibid: "In later texts the usual order is student, householder, hermit, and renouncer, reflecting the sequence of the passage from one _āśrama_ to another... In the Dharmasūtras, however, only Baudhāyana and Vasiṣṭha follow that order... A specific order becomes insignificant when the _āśramas_ are taken as four alternative adult vocations." 
Are they alternative adult vocations here in the Vṛṣasārasaṃgraha? They are numbered.

\textit{Gṛhastha. The Householder in Ancient Indian 
Religious Culture.} Edited by Patrick Olivelle. OUP, 2019.
Especially Csaba Dezső's article in it.

%N. of a celebrated king to whom Vaiśampāyana recited the [MBh.] (greatgrandson to Arjuna, as being son and, successor to Parikshit who was the son of Arjuna's son Abhimanyu) ["SBr.] xi, xīi AitBr. "Sāṅkhir. xvi [MBh.] &c.;

Bisschop 2018:2:
``The full text of the corpus was first published by Naraharinātha in 1998, while over the past few years several scholars have started to work on individual parts of the corpus or referred to them in their studies. See, in particular, Acharya 2009; Bisschop 2010, 2014; De Simini 2013, 2016a, 2016b, 2017; De Simini & Mirnig 2017; Goodall 2011; Kafle 2013, 2015; Magnone 2005; Sanderson 2003/04, 2012/13; Schwartz 2012. An edition of the Śivadharmaśāstra alone, based on a single manuscript in the Adyar Library, has been published more recently as well (Jugnu & Sharma 2014). The Śivopaniṣad, which also forms part of the Śivadharma corpus, was already published much earlier but was not recognised as such, being included in a collection of Upaniṣads (Kunhan Raja 1933).''

What MS did Naraharinātha used? See Biscchop 2018:58--59.

Vipula in the MBh:

	MBh 13040016aff

	Devaśarman and his wife Ruci
	13040017a tasya rūpeṇa --> 13040017a tasyā rūpeṇa

	all gods, esp. Indra, are in love with her
	but Devaśarman guards her
	wants to perform yajña: how to guard her during the ritual?
	calls his pupil, Vipula
	tells him that Indra can assume various forms
	Vipula decides that the only way to protect her from Indra is to magically 'enter' her (with yoga)
	he tells her stories and enters her 

	MBh 13041001ff
	Indra sees the opportunity and enters the āśrama as a beautiful man
	he sees Vipula's lifeless body
	Ruci fancies Indra, but Vipula in his body stops her from standing up
	Indra sings to her beautiful songs
	he says "I have come for you, I am Devendra, I am in love"
	Vipula stops her from doing anything
	Indra is a bit shocked by her not being moved, gets angry and can see now that Vipula is in her
	Vipula leaves her, enters his own body, and abuses Indra and tells Indra how wicked he is
	Indra is ashamed and disappears
	Devaśarman returns to the āśrama, Vipula tells him what happened and Devaśarman praises him

        ETC., see translation here:
        https://www.sacred-texts.com/hin/m13/m13b005.htm

        See summary also here:
        V. S. Sukthankar. Critical Studies in the Mahābhārata.
                Poona, V. S. Sukthankar Memorial Edition Committee, 1944. 317--318
        https://archive.org/details/in.ernet.dli.2015.281344/page/n333

<TEXT> susūkṣmam{ }avyaktajagatsusāram|</TEXT>
<APP>\vb <LEM>susūkṣma°</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; śusukṣma° \msCc</APP>
<APP>                <LEM>°jagatsusāram</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNc\Ed; °jagaśusāraṃ \msCc, °jagatsurāsuraṃ \msNb</APP>
        <!-- <TR> Having bowed to [Him] whose boundaries are limitless, 
             who has no beginning, no middle part and no end,
             [to Him] who is very subtle and who is the unmanifest and fine essence of the world,</TR> -->

<TEXT> harīndrabrahmādibhir{ }āptam{ }agraṃ</TEXT>
        <APP>\vc <LEM>°bhir{ }āpta°</LEM> \conj; °bhir{ }āsa° \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed</APP>

<TEXT> praṇamya vakṣye vṛṣasārasaṃgraham||</TEXT> 1.1
        <PARAL>ŁTestimonia for this chapter:    \msCa\ ff.\thinspace 193v--195v,
                                                \msCb\ ff.\thinspace 201v--203v,
                                                \msCc\ ff.\thinspace 267r--270r,
                                                \msNa\ ff.\thinspace 1v--3v,
                                                \msNb\ exp.\thinspace 43 (lower then upper leaf; 1.62cd--2.22 are missing),
                                                \msNc\ ff.\thinspace 209v--211v;
                                                \mssCaCbCc\ = \msCa + \msCb + \msCc; 
                                                lemmata in parentheses indicate a longer chuck of text for
                                                which there may be additional variants given in the apparatus$</PARAL>
% pranamya \msCc
                        % Nirajan na āptaṃ (the text)
                        % Flo ref to the text (Luca supports this)
                        % Judit would go for āsamagraṃ
                        % rhyme-wise āsamagraṃ (Alessandro) 
        <!-- <TR>[to Him] who is respected as the foremost by Hari, Indra, Brahmā and the other [gods],
             I shall recite [the work called] `A Compendium on the Essence of the Bull [of Dharma]'. </TR> -->
        <NOTE>ŁPāda$ a is reminiscent of, among other famous passages, Bhagavadgītā 11.19:
                        <br/>
                Łanādimadhyāntam anantavīryam <br/>
                anantabāhuṃ śaśisūryanetram / <br/>
                paśyāmi tvāṃ dīptahutāśavaktraṃ <br/>
                svatejasā viśvam idaṃ tapantam //$ <br/>
              This faint reference to the Bhagavadgītā seems proper at the
              beginning of a work that claims to deliver a teaching 
              based on, but also to surpass, the Mahābhārata (see following verses).
                See also e.g. Kūrmapurāṇa 1.11.237:<br/>
                        Łrūpaṃ tavośeṣakalāvihīnam [tavā? CHECK]<br/>
                        agocaraṃ nirmalam ekarūpam / <br/>
                        anādimadhyāntam anantām [anantam? CHECK] ādyaṃ  <br/>
                        namāmi satyaṃ tamasaḥ parastāt$ // <br/>
                To say that a god has no beginning and no end in a temporal or spacial
                sense is natural (Łanādi°...°antam$), but to have no `middle part'
                (Ł°madhya°$) in these senses is slightly less so.
                Thus the rather commonly occuring phrase Łanādimadhyāntam$ is probably not 
                much more than a fixed expression meaning `endless and/or eternal'.
                As to which god this stanza is referring to, it may be Śiva,
                his name not being listed among those who treat him as chief god,
                but the phrasing of the verse is vague enough to keep the question somewhat open:
                the impersonal Brahman might be another option, even more so if we
                look at 1.9--10, two verses nearby verses discussing Łbrahmavidyā$.
                
                <sep/>
                In Łpāda$ b Łjagat-susāraṃ$ is most probably not 
                to be interpreted as Łjagatsu sāraṃ$.
                <sep/>
                Strictly speaking, Łpāda$ c is unmetrical, but it is better to 
                simply acknowledge here the phenomenon of `muta cum liquida', namely
                that syllables followed by consonant clusters such as 
                Łra, bra, hra, kra, śra, śya, śva, sva, dva$ can be treated as short.
                Thus Łharīndrabrahmā°$ can be treated as a regular beginning
                of an Łupajāti$ (. - . - -), the syllable 
                Łbra$ not turning the previous syllable long.
                <sep/>
                The reading Łāsamagraṃ$ in Łpāda$ c is difficult to interpret. 
                The most tempting of all the possible corrections I have considered
                (Łarcyam/arhyam/arghyam/īḍyam agraṃ$)
                seemed to be Łāptam agraṃ$, meaning `appointed/received/respected
                [by Hari, Indra, Brahmā etc.] as the foremost one'.
                The fact that the Łakṣara$s Łāsam$ and Łāptam$ look similar
                in most of the scripts used in our manuscripts supports this
                conjecture.
                <sep/>
                Note how we could percieve the end of Łpāda$s a and b, 
                as well as Łpāda$s c and d as rhymes.
                <sep/>
                Is pāda d hypermetrical? It is actually a Łvaṃśastha$ (Łtriṣṭubh - jagatī$ change).
                </NOTE>

%Łpāragaṃ$ in \msNa\msNb: the same extra syllable as in pāda d.

<TEXT> dvaipāyanamukhodgīrṇaṃ dharmaṃ yat{ }tad{ }dvijottama|</TEXT>

                distinguishing between the `secret Dharma' mentioned in 1.4c and

                I decided to follow MS \msCb\ because I suspected that the two Dharmas
                hinted at are the same.</NOTE>

<SUBCHAPTER>kālapāśaḥ</SUBCHAPTER>
<TRSUBCHAPTER>The noose of death and time</TRSUBCHAPTER>
<TEXT> vigatarāga uvāca|*</TEXT>
        <!-- <TR>Vigatarāga spoke:</TR> -->

        % CHECK śmaśāna rules, who ... Kane v4 p189ff and p222, but especially 227ff !
<TEXT> dehī dehe kṣayaṃ yāte bhūjalāgniśivādibhiḥ|</TEXT>
        <!-- <TR>When the body disintegrates in the ground, in water, in fire or [is torn apart] by jackals 
                        and other [animals],</TR> -->
        <APP>\va <LEM>dehe kṣa°</LEM> \msCa\msCc\msNc; dehāt{ }kṣa° \msCb, dehakṣa° \msNa\msNb\Ed</APP>
        <APP>\vb <LEM>°śivādibhiḥ</LEM>  \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; °śivādibhi \msCc</APP>
        <PARAL>\vb Łcf.\ Kūrmapurāṇa 2.23.74: $ 
                atha kaścit{ }pramādena mriyate 'gniviṣādibhiḥ| 
                tasyāśaucaṃ vidhātavyaṃ kāryaṃ caivodakādikam||</PARAL>
        <NOTE>The word Ł°śivā°$ in Łpāda$ b is slightly suspect, and could be the result
                of metathesis, from Ł°viṣā°$ (`by poison'). Nevertheless, 
                jackals seems appropriate in this context, for they 
                are commonly associated with human corpses, death and the cremation ground.
                (see e.g. Ohnuma 2019) 
                (Reiko Ohnuma 2019 = Ohnuma, R. The Heretical, Heterodox Howl: 
                        Jackals in Pāli Buddhist Literature. Religions 2019, 10, 221.)</NOTE>

<TEXT> yamadūtaiḥ kathaṃ nīto nirālambo nirañjanaḥ||</TEXT> 1.11
        <!-- <TR>how is the supportless and spotless soul led [to the netherworld] by Yama's messengers?</TR> -->
<APP>\vc <LEM>°dūtaiḥ</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; °dūte \msCc</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>nīto</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc; nītvā \msCc, nītā \Ed</APP>
        <APP>\vd <LEM>nirañjanaḥ</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; nirañjana \msCc</APP>

% nirdeha not in Monier-Williams
<TEXT> kālapāśaiḥ kathaṃ baddho nirdehaś{ }ca kathaṃ vrajet|</TEXT>
        <!-- <TR>How is it bound by the nooses of death/time? And if it is bodiless, how can it move?</TR> -->
<APP>\va <LEM>°pāśaiḥ</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; °pāśe \msCc</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>baddho</LEM> \msCa\msCc\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; ba<UNCL>ddho</UNCL> \msCb</APP>
<APP>\vb <LEM>nirdehaś{ }ca</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; nirdahaḥ sa \msCc</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>vrajet</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNc\Ed; bhavet \msNb</APP>
        <NOTE>The word Łkāla$ has, as usual, a double meaning in this verse: a Łkālapāśa$
                        is both Yama's noose, and also the limitation caused by time, 
                        as becomes clear at the discussion
                        on the different time units in verses 1.11--31.</NOTE>

<TEXT> svargaṃ vā sa kathaṃ yāti nirdeho bahudharmakṛt|</TEXT>

                     reach heaven if it has no body?</TR> -->

                     One digit (Łkalā$) is twice a twinkling.</TR> -->

                        redefines Łkalā$ in harmony with the traditional

<TEXT> kalādviguṇitā kāṣṭhā kāṣṭhā vai triṃśatiḥ kalā|</TEXT>
        <!-- <TR>Two digits (Łkalā$) form one bit (3.2 seconds; Łkāṣṭhā$).
                 Thirty bits (Łkāṣṭhā$) is one digit (1.6 minutes; Łkalā$).</TR> -->
        <APP>\vb <LEM>kāṣṭhā vai triṃśatiḥ</LEM> \msCa\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; vai triṃśatā \msCb, kāṣṭhā vai triṃśati \msCc</APP>

<TEXT> triṃśatkalā muhūrtaś{ }ca mānuṣena dvijottama||</TEXT> 1.19
        <!-- <TR>Thirty digits (Łkalā$) make up one section (48 minutes; Łmuhūrta$) 
                                according to mankind, O great Brahmin.</TR> -->

<SUBCHAPTER>brahmāṇḍam</SUBCHAPTER>

<TEXT> vigatarāga uvāca|*</TEXT>

        <!-- <TR>How many eggs of Brahmā are there? And are its measurements available anywhere?</TR> -->

        <!-- <TR>How could I enumerate all the eggs of Brahmā, O Brahmin?</TR> -->
        <APP>\va <LEM>prasaṃkhyātuṃ</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNc; prasaṃsā tu \msNb, ca saṃkhyātuṃ \Ed</APP>

                but we should probably understand Łbrahmāṇḍānāṃ viśeṣān prasaṃkhyātuṃ...$</NOTE>
        % praśaṃsā is an interesting variant and it explains śakyā

        <!-- <TR>in the manner in which Brahmā taught Mātariśvan in the past, truthfully.</TR> -->
        <APP>\vc <LEM>purā°</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\msNc; mamā° \Ed</APP>
        <PARAL>\vcd Łcf. Brahmāṇḍapurāṇa 3.4.58cd: $ 
                        brahmā dadau śāstram{ }idaṃ purāṇaṃ mātariśvane</PARAL> 
        <NOTE>Note that in Łpāda$ d Łmātariśvan$ stands for the accusative Łmātariśvānaṃ$ or
                        the dative Łmātariśvane$ or the genitive Łmātariśvanaḥ$.
                        The claim that Brahmā taught Mātariśvan is confirmed in 1.64cd,
                        again using the nominative for the accusative, dative or genitive, and
                        also e.g. in Brahmāṇḍapurāṇa 3.4.58cd.</NOTE>


<TEXT> śivāṇḍābhyantareṇaiva †sarveṣām{ }iva bhūritāḥ†|</TEXT>
<APP>\vb <LEM>°ṣām{ }iva bhūritāḥ</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNc; °ṣām{ }eva bhūritāḥ \msCc,°ṣām{ }iva bhūritā \msNa, °ṣām{ }eva bhūriṇām \msNb,                                                                         °ṣām{ }eva bhūr imāṃ \Ed</APP>
                                                                 % phps bhūriṇām for bhūrīṇām? or bhūr -in?

<TEXT> daśanāma diśāṣṭānāṃ brahmāṇḍe kīrtitaṃ śṛṇu||</TEXT> 1.40
        <!-- <TR>The ten names [of cosmic rulers/worlds] associated with each of the 
                eight directions in Brahmā's Egg, inside Śiva's Egg, [...], are being taught now, listen.</TR> -->
        <APP>\vc <LEM>diśā°</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNc\Ed; śivā° \msNb</APP>
        <APP>\vd <LEM>kīrtitaṃ śṛṇu</LEM> \msCa\msCc\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; ya ca kīrtitam \msCb</APP>
        <NOTE>The cruxed Łpāda$ may have read Łsarveṣām eva pūjitāḥ$ originally 
                                        (`They are worshipped by all').
        <sep/>
        In Łpāda$ c, understand Łdiśāṣṭānāṃ$ as Łdiśām aṣṭānāṃ$ or Łdigaṣṭakānāṃ$</NOTE>



<SUBCHAPTER>daśa nāmāni digaṣṭakānām</SUBCHAPTER>
<TRSUBCHAPTER>The ten names (of cosmic rulers/worlds) associated with each of the eight directions</TRSUBCHAPTER>
% East
<TEXT> sahāsaha sahaḥ sahyo visahaḥ saṃhato 'sabhā|</TEXT>
        <!-- <TR>[1] Saha, [2] Asaha, [3] Sahas, [4] Sahya, [5] Visaha, [6] Saṃhata, [7] Asabhā,</TR> -->
<APP>\va <LEM>sahāsaha</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\Ed; sahāsahaḥ \msNc</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>sahyo</LEM> \msCa\msCc\msNa\msNb\msNc; sa<UNCL>hyo</UNCL> \msCb, sajño \Ed</APP>
<APP>\vb <LEM>visahaḥ</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; visaha \msCc</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>sabhā</LEM> \msCa\msCc\msNa\msNb\msNc; sabhāḥ \msCb, satā \Ed</APP>
        <NOTE>I chose to supply an Łavagraha$ before Łsabhā$ only because all the sources 
                consulted read Łsaṃhato$ as the previous word, making the Łsandhi$
                Ło-s$ suspicious. 
                Note that many of the names here and in the following verses are,
                in the absence of any parallel passage, rather insecure.
                What is clear here is that the names evoke the name Sahasrākṣa, one of the appellations of
                Indra, the guadrian of the eastern direction.</NOTE>

<TEXT> prasaho 'prasahaḥ sānuḥ pūrvato daśa nāyakāḥ||</TEXT> 1.41

<APP><LEM>prasahaḥ</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc; prasa<UNCL>vaḥ</UNCL> \msCc, saprahaḥ \Ed</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>sānuḥ</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb; sānu \msNc\Ed</APP>
        <APP>\vd <LEM>pūrvato</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\msNc; parvato \Ed</APP>

<TEXT> saṃyano yamanoyāno yaniyugmā yanoyanaḥ|</TEXT>
<APP>\va <LEM>saṃyano</LEM> \msNa; saṃyamo \mssCaCbCc\msNb\Ed, saṃyamā \msNc</APP>
<APP><LEM>yamano°</LEM> \msCa\msCc\msNa\msNc\Ed; yamuno° \msCb\msNb</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>°yāno</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\Ed; °yāmo \msNc</APP>
<APP>\vb <LEM>yano yanaḥ</LEM> \msNb; nayo yanaḥ \msCa\msCc\msNa, nayo nayaḥ \msCb,                                nayo yamaḥ \msNc, nayonaya \Ed</APP>
        <!-- <TR>[7] Saṃyana, [8] Yamanoyāna, [9] Yaniyugmā, [10] Yanoyana.</TR> -->
        <NOTE>I have choosen the variant Łsaṃyano$ in Łpāda$ c only to avoid the repetition of
                        the name Łsaṃyama$, and the variant Łyanoyanaḥ$ because I suspect that
                        most of the names here should begin with Łya$. All the name forms
                        in this verse are to be taken as tentative. The only 
                        guiding light is the presence of Łya$, reinforcing their connection with Yama.</NOTE>

<SUBCHAPTER>vyāsāḥ</SUBCHAPTER>
<TRSUBCHAPTER>The Redactors (of the Purāṇas)</TRSUBCHAPTER>
% purāṇāni? or aśī for aśīti 80?
% stem form?
<TEXT> purāṇāśīsahasrāṇi śatāni dvijasattama|</TEXT>
        <!-- <TR>O true Brahmin, the Purāṇa[s of] 8,000,000 [verses]</TR> -->
        <APP>\vb <LEM>°sattama</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNc\Ed; ××maḥ  \msCc</APP>
        <NOTE>ŁPāda$ a should probably be analysed and interpreted as 
                        Łpurāṇam (purāṇānām aśītisahasrāṇi śatāni ślokāni) brahmaṇā kathitam$.
                <sep/>
                Compare this list to Viṣṇupurāṇa 3.3.11--19:
                <sep/>
                Łdvāpare prathame vyastaḥ svayaṃ vedaḥ svayaṃbhuvā|<br/>
                dvitīye dvāpare caiva vedavyāsaḥ prajāpati||<br/>
                tṛtīye cośanā vyāsaś caturthe ca bṛhaspatiḥ| <br/>
                savitā pañcame vyāsaḥ ṣaṣṭhe mṛtyuḥ smṛtaḥ prabhuḥ|| <br/>
                saptame ca tathaivendro vasiṣṭhaś cāṣṭame smṛtaḥ| <br/>
                sārasvataś ca navame tridhāmā daśame smṛtaḥ|| <br/>
                ekādaśe tu triśikho bharadvājas tataḥ paraḥ| <br/>
                trayodaśe cāntarikṣo varṇī cāpi caturdaśe|| <br/>
                trayyāruṇaḥ pañcadaśe ṣoḍaśe tu dhanañjayaḥ| <br/>
                kratuñjayaḥ saptadaśe tadūrdhvaṃ ca jayaḥ smṛtaḥ|| <br/>
                tato vyāso bharadvājo bharadvājāc ca gautamaḥ| <br/>
                gautamād uttaro vyāso haryātmā yo 'bhidhīyate|| <br/>
                atha haryātmanonte ca smṛto vājaśravāmuniḥ| <br/>
                somaśuṣkāyaṇas tasmāt tṛṇabindur iti smṛtaḥ|| <br/>
                ṛkṣobhūdbhārgavas tasmād vālmīkir yo 'bhidhīyate| <br/>
                tasmād asmatpitā śaktir vyāsas tasmād ahaṃ mune|| <br/>
                jātukarṇo 'bhavan mattaḥ kṛṣṇadvaipāyanas tataḥ| <br/>
                aṣṭaviṃśatir ity ete vedavyāsāḥ purātanāḥ||  <br/>$
                <sep/>
                Another relevant passage is Brahmāṇḍapurāṇa 3.4.58cd--67:
                <sep/>
                Łbrahmā dadau śāstram idaṃ purāṇaṃ mātariśvane|| <br/>
                tasmāc cośanasā prāptaṃ tasmāc cāpi bṛhaspatiḥ|   <br/>
                bṛhaspatis tu provāca savitre tadanantaram||   <br/>
                savitā mṛtyave prāha mṛtyuś cendrāya vai punaḥ| <br/>
                indraś cāpi vasiṣṭāya so 'pi sārasvatāya cai|| <br/>
                sārasvatas tridhāmne 'tha tridhāmā ca śaradvate| <br/>
                śaradvāṃs tu triviṣṭāya so 'ntarikṣāya dattavān|| <br/>
                carṣiṇe cāntarikṣo vai so 'pi trayyāruṇāya ca| <br/>
                trayyāruṇād dhanañjayaḥ sa vai prādāt kṛtañjaye|| <br/>
                kṛtañjayāt tṛṇañjayo bharadvājāya so 'py atha|   <br/>
                gautamāya bharadvājaḥ so 'pi niryyantare punaḥ|| <br/>
                niryyantaras tu provāca tathā vājaśravāya vai|   <br/>
                sa dadau somaśuṣmāya sa cādāt tṛṇabindave|| <br/>
                tṛṇabindus tu dakṣāya dakṣaḥ provāca śaktaye| <br/>
                śakteḥ parāśaraś cāpi garbhasthaḥ śrutavānidam|| <br/>
                parāśarāj jātukarṇyas tasmād dvaipāyanaḥ prabhuḥ|   <br/>
                dvaipāyanāt punaś cāpi mayā prāptaṃ dvijottama|| <br/>
                mayā caitat punaḥ proktaṃ putrāyāmitabuddhaye|   <br/>
                ity eva vākyaṃ brahmādiguruṇāṃ samudāhṛtam||  $ <br/>
                </NOTE>

<TEXT> bhogam{ }akṣayas{ }tatraiva janmamṛtyur{ }na vidyate||</TEXT> 2.25
        <APP>\vc <LEM>bhogam{ }akṣayas{ }ta°</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc; bhogamayās{ }tu ta° \Ed</APP>
        <APP>\vd <LEM>°mṛtyur{ }na</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNc\Ed; °mṛtyu na \msNb</APP>
        <!-- <TR>I do not know the length and width of the Śivāṇḍa.
             Enjoyment is undecaying there, and there is no birth or death there.</TR> -->
        <NOTE>Pāda c is unmetrical, or rather, a ra-vipulā with licence 
                (tatraiva as SHORT-LONG). Note also the gender problem 
                (<i>bhogam akṣayas</i>), or rather take <i>-m-</i> as a sandhi-bridge 
                (<i>bhoga-m-akṣayas</i>, for <i>bhogo 'kṣayas</i>).</NOTE>

<TEXT> kimarthaṃ dharmam{ }ity{ }āhuḥ katimūrtiś{ }ca kīrtyate|</TEXT>
        <APP>\va <LEM>āhuḥ</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc; āhu \Ed</APP>
        <!-- <TR>Why do they call [Dharma] Dharma? And how many embodiments (Łmūrti$) is he known to have?</TR> -->
% NEW
        <NOTE>For the correct interpretation of Łpāda$ a, namely to decide whether these questions
                focus on the bull of Dharma or Dharma itself/himself, see MBh 12.110.10--11:
                <sep/>
                Łprabhāvārthāya bhūtānāṃ dharmapravacanaṃ kṛtam|<br/>
                 yat syād ahiṃsāsaṃyuktaṃ sa dharma iti niścayaḥ||<br/>
                 dhāraṇād dharma ity āhur dharmeṇa vidhṛtāḥ prajāḥ|<br/>
                 yat syād dhāraṇasaṃyuktaṃ sa dharma iti niścayaḥ||$
                <sep/>
                Note the similarities with this chapter: the phrase Łdharma ity āhur$,
                the fact that the present chapter from verse 18 on is actually a chapter on Łahiṃsā$,
                and that the etimological explanation involves the word [Łā$]Łdhāraṇa$ in
                both cases. These lead me to think that in Łpāda$s ab of this verse in the VSS,
                it is Dharma that is the focus of the inquiry and not the bull.</NOTE>

<TEXT> caturāśrama yo dharmaḥ kīrtitāni manīṣibhiḥ||</TEXT> 3.4
        <APP>\vc <LEM>caturā°</LEM> \msCb\msNa\msNb\Ed; cāturā° \msCa\msNc</APP>
        <!-- <TR>The four-legged Bull is the embodiment of both Śruti and Smṛti.
               The four Łāśrama$s are taught by the wise to be [the four legs of] Dharma.
                [or rather: ... which is Dharma as made up of the four āśramas... kīrtitaḥ!]</TR> -->
% only 3 āśramas here ! Judit ; CsK: maybe taṃ santo... is the fourth? NEW
% Olivelle Āśrama p55: "Elsewhere also in the same dialogue the author affirms "the unity of all the āśramas with
respect to their end" (12.262.34) and calls the āśramas the four feet of dharma (12.262.19,21)."
        <NOTE>A similar image of the legs of the Bull of Dharma being the four Łāśrama$s 
                is hinted at MBh 12.262.19--21: 
                <sep/>
                    Łdharmam{ }ekaṃ catuṣpādam{ }āśritās{ }te nararṣabhāḥ|
               <br/>taṃ santo vidhivat{ }prāpya gacchanti paramāṃ gatim||
               <br/>gṛhebhya eva niṣkramya vanam{ }anye samāśritāḥ|
               <br/>gṛham{ }evābhisaṃśritya tato 'nye brahmacāriṇaḥ||
               <br/>dharmam{ }etaṃ catuṣpādam{ }āśramaṃ brāhmaṇā viduḥ|
               <br/>ānantyaṃ brahmaṇaḥ sthānaṃ brāhmaṇā nāma niścayaḥ||$
        <sep/>
        On the more frequently quoted interpretation of the four legs, see Olivelle `Āśrama', 235:
        ``Dharma and truth possess all four feet and are whole during the Kṛta yuga, 
        and people did not obtain anything unrighteously (Ładharmeṇa$). 
        By obtaining, however, Łdharma$ has lost one foot during each of the other Łyuga$s 
        and righteousness (Łdharma$) likewise has diminished by one quarter due to theft, 
        falsehood, and deceit. (MDh 1.81--82)''
        <sep/>
        Understand Łpāda$s c and d as Łcatvāri āśramāṇi kīrtitāni dharmo manīṣibhiḥ$ or
                Łyo dharmaḥ kīrtitaś caturāśramāṇi manīṣibhiḥ$ or 
                Łyo dharmaś caturāśramaḥ kīrtito manīṣibhiḥ$.</NOTE>

        <NOTE>Again, I have chosen/applied the plural forms Ł°ādyāḥ$ and Łsumanoharāḥ$ in Łpāda$ b to hint at the fact
                        that the presence of the plural is to be preferred here; thus only Łviśālākṣī$ is

        [or 17 BhP.; or 8 R.] those of Kaśyapa, becoming by this latter the mothers 
        of gods, demons, men, and animals, while 10 are married to Dharma, Mn. ix, 128f.'' CHECK</NOTE>

<TEXT> śraddhā lakṣmīr{ }dhṛtis{ }tuṣṭiḥ puṣṭir{ }medhā kriyā lajjā|</TEXT>

        <NOTE>For Dharma's thirteen wives and their sons, see Liṅgapurāṇa 1.5.34-37 (note the 
                       similarity between the first line and VSS 3.6cd--7ab above):
        <sep/>
        Łdharmasya patnyaḥ śraddhādyāḥ kīrtitā vai trayodaśa|
        <br/>tāsu dharmaprajāṃ vakṣye yathākramam{ }anuttamam||
        <br/>kāmo darpo 'tha niyamaḥ saṃtoṣo lobha eva ca|
        <br/>śrutas{ }tu daṇḍaḥ samayo bodhaś{ }caiva mahādyutiḥ||
        <br/>apramādaś{ }ca vinayo vyavasāyo dvijottamāḥ|
        <br/>kṣemaṃ sukhaṃ yaśaś{ }caiva dharmaputrāś{ }ca tāsu vai|| 
        <br/>dharmasya vai kriyāyāṃ tu daṇḍaḥ samaya eva ca|
        <br/>apramādas{ }tathā bodho buddher{ }dharmasya tau sutau||$
        </NOTE>

<TEXT> buddhiḥ śāntir{ }vapuḥ kīrtiḥ siddhiḥ prasūtisambhavāḥ||</TEXT> 3.9
        <APP>\vc <LEM>buddhiḥ</LEM> \msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; buddhi \msCa</APP>
<APP>\vd <LEM>siddhiḥ prasūtisambhavāḥ</LEM> \conj; siddhiś{ }cābhūtisambhavā \msCa\msNa\msNb\msNc,                                        siddhiś{ }cātisambhavā \msCb, siddhiś{ }ca bhūtisambhavā \Ed</APP>
        <!-- <TR>[Dharma's wives are:] [1] Śraddhā (`Faith'), [2] Lakṣmī (`Prosperity'), [3] Dhṛti (`Resolution'), 
        [4] Tuṣṭi (`Satisfaction'), [5] Puṣṭi (`Growth'), [6] Medhā (`Wisdom'), 
        [7] Kriyā (`Ritual'), [8] Lajjā (`Modesty'), [9] Buddhi (`Intelligence'), 
        [10] Śānti (`Tranquillity'), [11] Vapus (`Beauty'), [12] Kīrti (`Fame'), [13] Siddhi (`Success'), 
                      [all] born to Prasūti [Dakṣa's wife].</TR> -->
        <NOTE><sep/>Łprasūtisambhavāḥ$ is a rather bold conjecture that can be supported by two facts:
                        firstly, the readings of the manuscripts are difficult to make sense of and thus are
                                         probably corrupt; secondly, a corruption from the name Prasūti, that of Dakṣa's wife, to Łābhūti$
                                         is relatively easily to explain, Łsū$ and Łbhū$ being close enough in some scripts 
                                         (e.g. in \msCa) to cause confusion. Another option would be to accept 
                                         Ābhūti as the name of Dakṣa's wife.
                <sep/>
                For Prasūti being Dakṣa's wife in other sources,
                         see e.g. Liṅgapurāṇa 1.5.20--21 (but also note the presence of the name Sambhūti...):
                               Łprasūtiḥ suṣuve dakṣāc{ }caturviṃśatikanyakāḥ|
                                śraddhāṃ lakṣmīṃ dhṛtiṃ puṣṭiṃ tuṣṭiṃ medhāṃ kriyāṃ tathā||
                                buddhi lajjāṃ vapuḥ śāntiṃ siddhiṃ kīrtiṃ mahātapāḥ|
                                khyātiṃ śāntiś{ }ca saṃbhūtiṃ smṛtiṃ prītiṃ kṣamāṃ tathā||$</NOTE>


        % \msCa\ 390.jpg
% stem form?
<TEXT> śraddhā kāmaḥ suto jāto darpo lakṣmīsutaḥ smṛtaḥ|</TEXT>
        <APP>\va <LEM>kāmaḥ</LEM> \msNa; kāma° \msCa\msCb\msNb\msNc, dharma° \Ed</APP>
        <!-- <TR>Śraddhā's son is Kāma (`Desire'), Darpa (`Pride') is said to be Lakṣmī's son.</TR> -->
        <NOTE>Understand Łśraddhā$ as a stem form noun for Łśraddhāyāḥ$ (gen./abl.).</NOTE>

<TEXT> dhṛtyās{ }tu niyamaḥ putraḥ saṃtoṣas{ }tuṣṭijaḥ smṛtaḥ||</TEXT> 3.10

        ity{ }eṣa vai sukhodarkaḥ sargo dharmasya kīrtitaḥ||</PARAL>

<TEXT> kriyāyās{ }tv{ }abhayaḥ putro daṇḍaḥ samaya eva ca||</TEXT> 3.11
        <APP>\vc <LEM>tv{ }abhayaḥ putro</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc; tūbhayaḥ putrau \Ed</APP>
<APP>\vd <LEM>daṇḍaḥ</LEM> \corr; daṇḍe \msCa\msNaacorr, daṇḍo \msCb, daṇḍa° \msNapcorr\msNb\msNc\Ed</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>ca</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc; tu \Ed</APP>
        <!-- <TR>Kriyā's sons are Abhaya (`Freedom from danger'), Daṇḍa (`Punishment') and Samaya (`Law').</TR> -->
        <NOTE>It is tempting to emend Łabhayaḥ$ to Łubhayaḥ$, thus matching the relevant line in the Kūrmapurāṇa cited above:
                        Łkriyāyāś cābhavat putro daṇḍaḥ samaya eva ca$ and allotting only two sons to Kriyā, but
                        in a number of sources Kriyā actually has three sons, see e.g. Viṣṇupurāṇa 1.7.29(ab? CHECK in book),
                        where they are named as Daṇḍa, Naya and Vinaya:
                                Łmedhā śrutaṃ kriyā daṇḍaṃ nayaṃ vinayam eva ca$. 
                        Perhaps read Łkriyāyās tu nayaḥ putro$ in pāda c? Compare Vāyupurāṇa 1.10.34cd
                                        Łkriyāyās tu nayaḥ prokto daṇḍaḥ samaya eva ca$ 
                                with Brahmāṇḍapurāṇa 1.9.60ab:
                                        Łkriyāyās tanayau proktau damaś ca śama eva ca$</NOTE>

<TEXT> lajjāyāḥ sudhiyaḥ putra apramādaś{ }ca tāv{ }ubhau||</TEXT> 3.12
<APP>\vc <LEM>sudhiyaḥ</LEM> \Ed; sudhiya \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>putra</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc; putraḥ \Ed</APP>

        <!-- <TR>Lajjā has two [more] sons: Sudhiya[/Sudhī] (`Wise') and Apramāda (`Cautiousness').
                [or one more son only: the wise Apramāda?]</TR> -->
        <NOTE>In a very similar passages in Kūrmapurāṇa 1.8.20 ff., Apramāda is Buddhi's son and 
                 Lajjā has only one son, Vinaya. In the above verse (VSS 3.12), Łsudhiyaḥ$ (for Łsudhīḥ$) may only be 
                        qualifying Łapramāda$, thus Lajjā may have two sons: Vinaya and the wise Apramāda.</NOTE>

<TEXT> kautūhalam{ }atīvaṃ me kartaya jñānasaṃśayam||</TEXT> 3.14
<APP>\vc <LEM>kautūhala°</LEM> \msCa\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; kotūhala° \msCb</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>°tīvaṃ me</LEM> \msCa\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; °tīva me \msCb</APP>
<APP>\vd <LEM>kartaya</LEM> \eme; kīrtaya \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>°saṃśayam</LEM> \msCa\msNa\msNc\Ed; °saṃśayaḥ \msCb\msNb</APP>
                <!-- <TR>I am extremely intrigued. Cut my doubts concerning [this] knowledge.</TR> -->
        <NOTE>Note Łdharma$ as a neuter noun and the form Łatīvaṃ$ for Łatīva$ metri causa. 
        My emendation from Łkīrtaya$ (`declare') to Łkartaya$ (`cut') was influenced by the combination
        of Łchindhi$ and Łsaṃśaya$, often with Łkautūhala$, elsewhere in the VSS:
                3.2b: Łsaṃśayaṃ chindhi tattvataḥ$; 
                10.XXcd: Łkautūhalaṃ mahaj jātaṃ chindhi saṃśayakārakam$;
                15.2ab: Łetat kautūhalaṃ chindhi saṃśayaṃ parameśvara$. 
                The reading Łkīrtaya$ may have been the result of the influence of Łkīrtitā$ in 3.13b above 
                       (De Simini's convinicing observation).</NOTE>

% BANDHA PEOPLE
<TEXT> baddhvā pādau bhujoraś{ }ca śirorukkaṇṭhapāśitāḥ|</TEXT> 
        <APP>\va <LEM>bhujoraś{ }ca</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNb\msNc; bhujauraś{ }ca \msNa\Ed</APP>

        <!-- <TR>[Others,] tie up [people] at their feet and their arms and chests.
                [These,] bound by [with?] their hair and [on their?] necks,</TR> -->
        <NOTE>Understand Łbhujoraś ca$ in Łpāda$ a as Łbhuje urasi ca$, in this case with an instance of double sandhi:
                Łbhuje urasi ca$ -- Łbhuja urasi ca$ -- Łbhujorasi ca$. Alternatively, understand it as a compound:     
                                Łbhujorasi$.</NOTE>

<APP>\vc <LEM>hiṃsakāny{ }āhu vi°</LEM> \msCb\msNb\msNc;                                hiṃsakāny{ }āhur{ }vi° \msCa\msNa\ \unmetr, hiṃsakety{ }āhu vi° \Ed</APP>

<TEXT> nātaḥ parataraṃ duḥkhaṃ nātaḥ parataro 'yaśaḥ||</TEXT> 3.25
        <!-- <TR>There is no greater suffering or greater infamy.</TR> -->
        <NOTE>Note that Łparataro$ is masculine in Łpāda$ d, picking up a neuter Ł'yaśaḥ$.
        This phenomenon is probably the result of Ł'yaśaḥ$ resembling a masculine noun ending in Ł-aḥ$
                and also of the metrical problem with the grammatically correct
                        Łnātaḥ parataram ayaśaḥ$.</NOTE>

<TEXT> nātaḥ parataraṃ pāpaṃ nātaḥ parataraṃ viṣam|</TEXT>

                Łnātaḥ paraṃ tapo ...$ (`There is no greater austerity...').</NOTE>

        <APP>\vc <LEM>guṇākarṣā°</LEM> \conjTorzsok; guṇākāśā° \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\msNc, guṇā kuryā° \Ed</APP>
       <!-- <TR> in order to be able to draw a bow with force,
                        or out of fear of the danger coming from the enemy.</TR> -->
        <NOTE>Łguṇākāśāt$ in pāda c is difficult to interpret and 
                Łguṇākarṣāt$ is a conjecture by Judit Törzsök which fits the context well,
                although the polysemy of Łguṇa$ may allow for other solutions.
        <sep/>
        Verses 3.40--42 may be echoing Brahmapurāṇa 216.64--66:
        <sep/>
                      Ł māṃsān miṣṭataraṃ nāsti bhakṣyabhojyādikeṣu ca |<br/>
                        tasmān māṃsaṃ na bhuñjīta nāsti miṣṭaiḥ sukhodayaḥ || <br/>
                        gosahasraṃ tu yo dadyād yas tu māṃsaṃ na bhakṣayet |<br/>
                        samāv etau purā prāha brahmā vedavidāṃ varaḥ ||<br/>
                        sarvatīrtheṣu yat puṇyaṃ sarvayajñeṣu yat phalam |<br/>
                        amāṃsabhakṣaṇe viprās tac ca tac ca ca tatsamam || $
        </NOTE>

%      - -     -  .  . -?   . -  . . . -    -  - .  -  -  . -
% unmetrical
<TEXT> etatpuṇyaphalam{ }ahiṃsakajanaḥ prāpnoti niḥsaṃśayaḥ||</TEXT> 3.42
<APP>\vd <LEM>°phalam{ }ahiṃsa°</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\Ed; °phalaṃ tv{ }ahiṃsa° \msNc</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>niḥsaṃśayaḥ</LEM> \msCc\msNa\msNb\msNc; ¤¤¤¤ \msCa, niḥsaṃśaya¤ \msCb, niḥsaṃśayaṃ \Ed</APP>
        % nissaṃśayaḥ \msCc, niḥsaṃśayaḥ \msNa
        <!-- <TR>A person who refrains from violence will gain,
                 no doubt about it, the [same] meritorious rewards
                 that others would get by donating the three worlds filled with jewels and 
                 gems in their entirety to an excellent Brahmin,
                 by performing a thousand [times] ten trillion (Łpadma$)
                 [times] ten thousand (Łayuta$) Łkoṭīyajña$ (= koṭihoma?) sacrifices,
                 by donating the earth [to a priest] as sacrificial fee,
                 and by bathing [at] a thousand times ten million times a million (Łniyuta$) 
                                                sacred places at once,</TR> -->
        <NOTE>On Łpadma$ meaning `ten trillion', and on other words for numbers, see 1.32--35. 
        <sep/>
                Łkoṭīyajña$ in pāda d may refer to a special kind of sacrifice, 
                mostly known as Łkoṭihoma$ in the Purāṇas and in inscriptions 
                (see e.g. Fleming 2010 and 2013)
                It probably involves a hundred fire-pits 
                and a hundred times one thousand brāhmaṇas (hence the name `the ten-million sacrifice').
                See e.g. Bhaviṣyapurāṇa uttaraparvan 4.142.54--58:
                        <sep/>
                             Ł  śatānano daśamukho dvimukhaikamukhas tathā |
                                caturvidho mahārāja koṭihomo vidhīyate || 
                                kāryasya gurutāṃ jñātvā naiva kuryād aparvaṇi |
                                yathā saṃkṣepataḥ kāryaḥ koṭihomas tathā śṛṇu ||
                                kṛtvā kuṇḍaśataṃ divyaṃ yathoktaṃ hastasaṃmitam |
                                ekaikasmiṃs tataḥ kuṇḍe śataṃ viprān niyojayet ||
                                sadyaḥ pakṣe tu viprāṇāṃ sahasraṃ parikīrtitam |
                                ekasthānapraṇīte 'gnau sarvataḥ paribhāvite || 
                                homaṃ kuryur dvijāḥ sarve kuṇḍe kuṇḍe yathoditam |
                                yathā kuṇḍabahutve 'pi rājasūye mahākratau ||  $
                        <sep/>
                Note that the second syllable of Łphalam$ in Łpāda$ d is treated as a long syllable: this
                happens often at word-boundaries in this text; and 
                note how \msNc\ aims to restore the metre by inserting Łtv$ after its Łphalaṃ$.</NOTE>

Fleming, Benjamin J. `New copperplate grant of Śrīcandra (no. 8) from
Bangladesh' BSOAS Volume 73, Issue 02 (June 2010, pp. 223--244)).
Peter Bisschop studied the kotihoma !
                        
Fleming, Benjamin J. `Making Land Sacred: Inscriptional Evidence for Buddhist Kings and Brahman Priests in Medieval Bengal' Numen 60.5-6 (2013) 559–85.

<TEXT> tiṣṭhanti sāgarāḥ satye samayena priyavrataḥ|</TEXT>
        <APP>\va <LEM>sāgarāḥ</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; sāgarā \msCc</APP>
        <APP>\vb <LEM>samayena</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\msNc; satyena ca \Ed</APP>
        <!-- <TR>The oceans dwell in Truth because of their encounter[?] with Priyavrata [Manu's son].</TR> -->
        <NOTE>ŁPāda$ b, Łsamayena priyavrataḥ$, probably stand for Łsamayena priyavratasya$ although
        it is unclear to me what exactly Łsamaya$ refers to here.
        <sep/>
        For Priyavrata's story, in which he wanted to turn nights into days by 
        circling aroung Mount Meru in a chariot, and by this produced the seven oceans,
         see e.g. Bhāgavatapurāṇa 5.1.30--31:<br/> 
        Łyāvad avabhāsayati suragirim anuparikrāman bhagavān ādityo
         vasudhātalam ardhenaiva pratapaty ardhenāvacchādayati, tadā hi [priyavrataḥ]
         bhagavadupāsanopacitātipuruṣaprabhāvas tad anabhinandan samajavena
         rathena jyotirmayena rajanīm api dinaṃ kariṣyāmīti saptakṛtvas 
         taraṇim anuparyakrāmad dvitīya iva pataṅgaḥ|
         ye vā u ha tadrathacaraṇanemikṛtaparikhātās te sapta sindhava āsan
         yata eva kṛtāḥ sapta bhuvo dvīpāḥ|$ 
        </NOTE>
        (see translation on pp.640 ff)

Is the Bhāgavatapurāṇa not too late? Priyavrata not in the epics. Mentioned in the Devībhāgavata/mahāpurāṇa
%Cf.\ Purāṇic Encyclopedia, s.v. Łpriyavrata$:
%``Priyavrata once circled round Meru in his chariot. As
%if competing with the Sun Priyavrata started his circling
%along with sunrise and ended it at sunset. He did so
%seven days making nights look like day and the sun
%appear dim and faded. The Sun was dejected and it
%was at the request of the Trimūrtis that Priyavrata
%stopped his circumambulation. It was the path of Priyavrata's
%circling for seven days that later became the seven
%oceans of Purāṇic fame. (Yuddha Kāṇḍa, Kamba Rāmāyaṇa).''

% \msCc\ 191.jpg
% MA-vipulā;   the plural vindhyās is a bit odd
% Judit the vidhya is the vardhamāna... there is a story with Agastya
<TEXT> satyena vindhyās{ }tiṣṭhanti vardhamāno na vardhate||</TEXT> 4.13
<APP>\vc <LEM>vindhyās{ }tiṣṭhanti</LEM> \msCa\msNa\msNc;                        vindhyas{ }tiṣṭhanti \msCb\msNb, vindhyā tiṣṭhanti \msCc, tiṣṭhate vindhyo \Ed</APP>
        <!-- <TR>It is because of Truth that the Vindhya mountain stands in place and that
                although is was growing it is not growing [anymore].</TR> -->
        <NOTE><sep/>Pādas cd refer to the story of Agastya and the Vindhya mountain:
        Vindhya became jealous of the Sun's revolving around Mount Meru and when the Sun 
        refused to him the same favour, he decided to grow higher and obstruct the Sun's movement.
        As a solution to this situation, 
        Agastya asked Vidhya to bend down to make it easier for him to reach the south and
        to remain thus until he retured. Vindhya agreed to do what Agastya asked him to do 
        but Agastya never returned. See Mahābhārata 3.102.1--14 (see in the word Łsamaya$ in verse 13
                and compare it to VSS 4.12b):
                <sep/>
        Ł                yudhiṣṭhira uvāca |<br/>
                         kimarthaṃ sahasā vindhyaḥ pravṛddhaḥ krodhamūrchitaḥ |<br/>
                         etad icchāmy ahaṃ śrotuṃ vistareṇa mahāmune ||<br/>
                         lomaśa uvāca |<br/>
                         adrirājaṃ mahāśailaṃ meruṃ kanakaparvatam |<br/>
                         udayāstamaye bhānuḥ pradakṣiṇam avartata ||<br/>
                         taṃ tu dṛṣṭvā tathā vindhyaḥ śailaḥ sūryam athābravīt |<br/>
                         yathā hi merur bhavatā nityaśaḥ parigamyate ||<br/>
                         pradakṣiṇaṃ ca kriyate mām evaṃ kuru bhāskara |<br/>
                         evam uktas tataḥ sūryaḥ śailendraṃ pratyabhāṣata ||<br/>
                         nāham ātmecchayā śaila karomy enaṃ pradakṣiṇam |<br/>
                         eṣa mārgaḥ pradiṣṭo me yenedaṃ nirmitaṃ jagat ||<br/>
                         evam uktas tataḥ krodhāt pravṛddhaḥ sahasācalaḥ |<br/>
                         sūryācandramasor mārgaṃ roddhum icchan paraṃtapa ||<br/>
                         tato devāḥ sahitāḥ sarva eva; sendrāḥ samāgamya mahādrirājam |<br/>
                         nivārayām āsur upāyatas taṃ; na ca sma teṣāṃ vacanaṃ cakāra ||<br/>
                         athābhijagmur munim āśramasthaṃ; tapasvinaṃ dharmabhṛtāṃ variṣṭham |<br/>
                         agastyam atyadbhutavīryadīptaṃ; taṃ cārtham ūcuḥ sahitāḥ surās te ||<br/>
                         devā ūcuḥ |<br/>
                         sūryācandramasor mārgaṃ nakṣatrāṇāṃ gatiṃ tathā |<br/>
                         śailarājo vṛṇoty eṣa vindhyaḥ krodhavaśānugaḥ ||<br/>
                         taṃ nivārayituṃ śakto nānyaḥ kaś cid dvijottama |<br/>
                         ṛte tvāṃ hi mahābhāga tasmād enaṃ nivāraya ||<br/>
                         lomaśa uvāca |<br/>
                         tac chrutvā vacanaṃ vipraḥ surāṇāṃ śailam abhyagāt |<br/>
                         so 'bhigamyābravīd vindhyaṃ sadāraḥ samupasthitaḥ ||<br/>
                         mārgam icchāmy ahaṃ dattaṃ bhavatā parvatottama |<br/>
                         dakṣiṇām abhigantāsmi diśaṃ kāryeṇa kena cit ||<br/>
                         yāvadāgamanaṃ mahyaṃ tāvat tvaṃ pratipālaya |<br/>
                         nivṛtte mayi śailendra tato vardhasva kāmataḥ ||<br/>
                         evaṃ sa samayaṃ kṛtvā vindhyenāmitrakarśana |<br/>
                         adyāpi dakṣiṇād deśād vāruṇir na nivartate ||<br/>
                         etat te sarvam ākhyātaṃ yathā vindhyo na vardhate |<br/>
                         agastyasya prabhāvena yan māṃ tvaṃ paripṛcchasi || $
</NOTE>

% nikṣepa as pawn ? Judit 
% vyāja is interest? Nirajan (Arthaśāstra)
ITTEN
Tr. by R. Shamasastry (1956):
2.6.10: mūlyaṃ bhāgo vyājī parighaḥ kḷptam rūpikam atyayaś cāyamukham|
`Capital (mūla), share (bhāga), premia (vyāji), parigha[2](?), fixed taxes (kḷpta), premia on coins (rūpika[3]) and fixed fines (atyaya) are several forms of revenue (āyamukha, i.e. the mouth from which income is to issue).'
2.6.22: vikriye paṇyānām arghavṛddhir upajā, mānaunmānaviśeṣo vyājī, krayasaṃgharṣe vārdhavṛddhiḥ - ity āyaḥ ||
`The rise in price of merchandise due to the use of different weights[6] and measures in selling is termed vyājī; the enhancement of price due to bidding among buyers is also another source of profit.'
vyāji = 'Transaction tax ' ( https://www.gktoday.in/gk/kautilyas-system-of-tax-administration )

<TEXT> stenastulya na mūḍham{ }asti puruṣo dharmārthahīno 'dhamaḥ</TEXT>
<APP>\va <LEM>stenastulya</LEM> \Ed; stenatulya \msCa\msCb\msNc\ \unmetr, steyastulya \msCc,                         steyatulya \msNa\ \unmetr, tena tulya \msNb\ \unmetr</APP>
        <NOTE>Understand Łstenastulya na mūḍham{ }asti$ (the reading of \Ed!) as a `metri causa' version of
                Łstenatulyo na mūḍho 'sti$, and see a similar case of a nominative ending
                inside of compound in pāda c below. One major concern remains here:
                the accepted reading here is that of \Ed, an edition that rarely emerges as 
                the sole transmitter of the best reading. A solution could be 
                to emend to Łstenaṃtulya...$, meaning `There is no bigger foolishness than theft',
                but then the second part of pāda a is difficult to connect. 
                <sep/> </NOTE>
        <!-- <TR>There are no bigger idiots than thieves, who are wicked people without Dharma and Artha.</TR> -->

<TEXT> prāptaḥśāsana tīvrasahyaviṣamaṃ prāpnoti karmeritaḥ</TEXT>
<APP>\vc <LEM>prāptaḥ°</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNb\msNc\Ed; prāpta° \msNa</APP>
<APP><LEM>°sahya°</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNc; ×× \msNb, °sadya° \Ed</APP>
<APP><LEM>°viṣamaṃ</LEM> \eme; °viṣamaḥ \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNc\Ed, ××× \msNb</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>karmeritaḥ</LEM> \msCb\msCc\msNa\msNc\Ed; karme<UNCL>rita</UNCL> \msCa, ××<UNCL>ritaḥ</UNCL> \msNb</APP>
        <!-- <TR>Having received his punishment, he gets into severe and [in]tolerable
                                 difficulties, propelled by [his] karma.</TR> -->
        <NOTE>Understand Łprāptaḥśāsana tīvrasahyaviṣamaṃ$ in pāda c as Łprāptaśāsanas tīvram asahyaṃ ca viṣamaṃ prāpnoti$.
                Alternatively, understand Łtīvrasahya°$ as Łduḥsahya°$ (suggested by Törzsök).
                <sep/>
                The actual reading of \msCa, Łprāptaś$ (lost in the process of normalization and standing
                        in contrast with that of all other MSS that read Łprāptaḥ$) may suggest
                        a doubling of the Łś$ of Łśāsana$ metri causa (suggestion by Törzsök).
                        More likely is that a licence of having a nominative ending inside of a compound
                        is applied here, as probably above in pāda a (also remarked by Törzsök).</NOTE>

<SUBCHAPTER>yameṣv{ }ānṛśaṃsyam (4)</SUBCHAPTER>
<TRSUBCHAPTER>The fourth yama-rule: Absence of cruelty</TRSUBCHAPTER>

<ANUSTUBH/>
<TEXT> aṣṭamūrtiśivadveṣṭā pitur{ }mātuś{ }ca yo dviṣet|</TEXT>
        <APP>\va <LEM>°śiva°</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\Ed; °śivaṃ \msNc</APP>
        <!-- <TR>The one who is hostile towards the eight-formed Śiva, he who hurts his mother or father,</TR> -->

% not absence of sandhi
<TEXT> gavāṃ vā atither{ }dveṣṭā nṛśaṃsāḥ pañca eva te||</TEXT> 4.31
<APP>\vc <LEM>gavāṃ vā</LEM> \msCa\msCc\msNa\msNc\Ed; avām{ }vā \msCb, ¤¤<UNCL>m{ }vā</UNCL> \msNb</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>atither{ }dve°</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNb\msNc\Ed; atithidve° \msCc, atithe dve° \msNa</APP>
        <APP>\vd <LEM>nṛśaṃsāḥ</LEM> \msCa\msCc\msNa\msNb; nṛśaṃsā \msCb\msNc\Ed</APP>
        <!-- <TR>he who is hostile towards cows or guests: these are the five types of cruel people.</TR> -->
        <NOTE>Note Łpitur$ and Łmātur$ used as accusative forms in Łpāda$ b, or alternatively
                        understand: `who are hateful towards their fathers and mothers'.</NOTE>

<TEXT> aṣṭamūrtiḥ śivaḥ sākṣāt{ }pañcavyomasamanvitaḥ|</TEXT>
        <APP>\va <LEM>°mūrtiḥ</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\msNc; °mūrti° \Ed</APP>
        <APP>\vb <LEM>°nvitaḥ</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNc\Ed; °nvitāḥ \msCc\msNb</APP>
MW: aṣṭamūrti (m.) ‘eight-formed’, a N. of Śiva (as identified with the five elements, mind, egotism, and Prakṛti [matter]; or, according to the opening of the Śakuntalā, with the five elements, the sun and moon and the sacrificing priest), [MBh. iii, 1939]; [Ragh. &c.] 

% Judit sa nṛśaṃsakaḥ --> tadnṛśaṃsakaḥ ? REVISE
<TEXT> sūryaḥ somaś{ }ca dīkṣaś{ }ca dūṣakaḥ tannṛśaṃsakaḥ||</TEXT> 4.32
<APP>\vc <LEM>sūryaḥ</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa; <UNCL>sūrya°</UNCL> \msNb\msNc, sūrya° \Ed</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>dīkṣa°</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNc; <UNCL>dī</UNCL>¤ \msNb, dīkṣu° \Ed</APP>
        <APP>\vd <LEM>tannṛ°</LEM> \emeTorzsok; sa nṛ° \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed</APP>
        <!-- <TR>Śiva in his manifest form (Łsākṣāt$) is of eight forms, with the five elements (vyoman! NOTE),
                and the Sun, the Moon, and the sacrificer. [He who] disgraces [any of these] is a cruel person.</TR> -->
        <NOTE>See Śakuntalā 1.1:
        <sep/>
                        Łyā sṛṣṭiḥ sraṣṭur ādyā [1] vahati vidhihutaṃ yā havir [2] yā ca hotrī [3]<br/>
                        ye dve kālaṃ vidhattaḥ [4,5] śruti-viṣaya-guṇā yā [6] sthitā vyāpya viśvam |<br/>
                        yām āhuḥ sarva-bīja-prakṛtir [7] iti yayā prāṇinaḥ prāṇavantaḥ [8]<br/>
                        pratyakṣābhiḥ prapannas tanubhir avatu vas tābhir aṣṭābhir īśaḥ ||$<br/>
                        <br/>
                 The eight Łtanu$s here are: [1] jala [2] agni [3] yajamāna [4,5] sūrya + candra [6] ākāśa [7] bhūmi [8] vāyu
                <sep/>
                For a similar interpretation of Łaṣṭamūrti$, see e.g. Īśānaśivagurudevapaddhati 2.29.34 (Łmantrapāda$;
                                note Łyajamāna$ for our Łdīkṣa$):
                                        Łkṣmā-vahni-yajamānārka-jala-vāyv-indu-puṣkaraiḥ$|
                                        Łaṣṭābhir mūrtibhiḥ śambhor dvitīyāvaraṇaṃ smṛtam$||
                (For Łpuṣkara$ as `sky, atmosphere', see e.g. Amarakośa 1.2.167:
                        Łdyodivau dve striyām abhraṃ vyoma puṣkaram ambaram$.)
                A closely related Aṣṭamūrti-hymn appears in Niśv mukha 1.30--41 (I owe thanks to Nirajan Kafle
                        for drawing my attention to this); see Kafle 2018: 62, 63, 116, 119. Kafle notes
                        that this hymn is closely parallel to some passages in the Prayogamañjarī (1.19--26),
                        the Tantrasamuccaya (1.16--23), and the Īśānaśivagurudevapaddhati (kriyāpāda 26.56--63). 
                                       See also TAK I s.v. Łaṣṭamūrti$.</NOTE>

                        Ł... etc.$

gomātṛśālāṃ yaḥ kuryāt sudṛḍhāmiṣṭakāmayīṃ ||
mṛtpiṇḍakāṣṭhākuḍyaṃ vā satkapāṭārgalānvitāṃ || 12.106 ||
sapidhānairgavākṣaiś ca caturbhiḥ samalaṅkṛtāṃ ||
paścāt kuḍyaṃ ca kartavyam uccair eva gavākṣakaṃ || 12.107 ||
supakkvair vaṃśavidalaiḥ kaṭaṃ kṛtvā samaṃdṛḍhaṃ ||
gavām adhaḥ pradātavyaṃ yena mūtram adho vrajet || 12.108 ||
gomayaṃ taṅgavākṣaiś ca kṣiptvā dūraṃ punar nnayet ||
vikīrya bhasmanā sarvaṃ dhūpayīta samantataḥ || 12.109 ||
kṛśavṛddhāturāṇāṃ ca pṛthagbhāvaṃ prakalpayet ||
pṛthak ca bālavatsānām itareṣāṃ pṛthagbhavet || 12.110 ||
damyānaḍvāha ya prāṇāṃ (vṛṣāṇāṃ) vasatiṃ kalpayet pṛthak ||
gāḥ (gāvaḥ) svapanti sukhenaiva vasanti ca ramanti ca || 12.111 ||
rudro brahmā ca viṣṇuś ca gośālāyāṃ sadā yajet ||
vinyasya pūrvavadanāṃ paścāt kuḍayagavākṣake || 12.112 ||
saṃhārūḍhāṃ mahādurgāṃ pañca nandādimātaraḥ ||
vighneśaṃ somanandiṃ ca pūrvādārabhya vinyaset || 12.113 ||
nadyubhaya taṭāśvattha vilvavalmīkamṛttikāṃ ||
pañcagavyena saṃyojya devān piṇḍaiḥ prakalpayet || 12.114 ||
dvāraśākhāgavākṣābhyāṃ mṛdākāryau suśobhanau ||
nandīśvaramahākālau vṛṣaṃ coccair vahiḥ sthitaṃ || 12.115 ||
yādṛśo'yaṃ vṛṣendraḥ syāt sarvāvayavaśobhanaḥ || 
taddarśanānubhāvena gavāṃ syustādṛśāḥ prajāḥ || 12.116 ||
aṣṭamyāṃ snāpanaṃ kṛtvā bhojanaṃ ca prakāmataḥ ||
svasti vācyaṃ tataś cāgre pradhānāṅgāmpraveśayet || 12.117 ||
iti yaḥ kārayed bhaktyā gomātṛgṛhamīdṛśaṃ ||
sa vidhūyāśu pāpāni śivaloke mahīyate || 12.118 ||

<TEXT> gavāhnikaṃ yaś{ }ca karoti nityaṃ</TEXT>
<APP>\va <LEM>gavāhnikaṃ</LEM> \msCb\msCc\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; gavāṃhnikaṃ \msCa</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>yaś{ }ca karoti</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\msNc; yaḥ prakaroti \Ed</APP>
        <!-- <TR>He who never fails to serve the cow daily [e.g. with a handful of grass], </TR> -->
% Nirajan giving a handful of grass offering
% MW on gavāhnika


11:07:09 From Kengo Harimoto To Everyone : 
Vācaspatyam
skandapurāṇe 
tṛṇāni khādanti vasanty araṇye pibanti toyāny aparigrahāṇi.
duhyanti vāhyanti punanti pāpaṃ gavāṃ rasair jīvati jīvalokaḥ.
tuṣṭās tu gāvaḥ śamayanti pāpaṃ dattās tu gāvas tridivan nayanti.
saṃrakṣitāś copanayanti tittaṃ 
gobhir na tulyaṃ dhanam asti kiñcit.
śaṣpaṃ samaśnāti dadāti nityaṃ 
pāpakṣatiṃ mitravivardhanaṃ ca.
sa eva cāryaiḥ paribhujyate ca 
gobhirna tulyaṃ dhanamasti kiñcit.
tṛṇāni śuṣkāṇi vane caritvā 
pītvāpi toyāny amṛtaṃ sravanti.
yadgomayādyāś ca punanti lokān 
gobhir na tulyaṃ dhanam asti kiñcit.

Revise!
CsK: but this is in the Śivadharmottara!

<TEXT> putradārātmanā vāpi yo 'tithim{ }anupūjayet|</TEXT>

                these influenced my decision to emend Ł°ātmano$ to Ł°ātmanā$ in pāda a.</NOTE>

<TEXT> puṇḍarīkasahasraṃ ca sarvatīrthatapaḥphalam||</TEXT> 4.45
        <APP>\vd <LEM>°tapaḥ°</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\Ed; °tapa° \msNc\ \unmetr</APP>
        <!-- <TR>a thousand Puṇḍarīka sacrifices and the fruit of [visiting] all the pilgrimage places and
                [performing] all the austerities;</TR> -->
                        % tapas at the tīrthas? Florinda
        See Mahābhārata Supp 14.4.1624 ff:
                                <skt>
                                bhagavān uvāca|<br/>
                                śṛṇuṣvāvahito rājan puṣpāṇi prītikṛnti me|<br/>
                                kumudaṃ karavīraṃ ca caṇakaṃ campakaṃ tathā||<br/>
                                mallikājātipuṣpaṃ ca nandyāvartaṃ ca nandikam|<br/>
                                palāśapuṣpapatrāṇi dūrvā bhṛṅgakam eva ca||<br/>
                                vanamālā ca rājendra matpriyāṇi viśeṣataḥ|<br/>
                                sarveṣām api puṣpāṇāṃ sahasraguṇam utpalam||<br/>
                                tasmāt padmaṃ tathā rājan padmāt tu śatapatrakam|<br/>
                                tasmāt sahasrapatraṃ tu puṇḍarīkaṃ tataḥ param||<br/>
                                <b>puṇḍarīkasahasrāt</b> tu tulasī guṇato 'dhikā|</skt>

<TEXT> atithir{ }yasya tuṣyeta nṛśaṃsamatam{ }utsṛjet|</TEXT>
<APP>\vb <LEM>nṛśaṃsamatam{ }utsṛjet</LEM> \msCa\msNa\msNc; nṛśaṃsamata utsṛjet \msCb,                             nṛśaṃsakamamam{ }utsṛjet \msCc, nṛsasamatam{ }utsṛjet \msNb, na saṃśaya samaśnute \Ed</APP>
        <!-- <TR>he whose guest is satisfied [and] he who can abandon the sentiment of cruelty,</TR> -->
        <NOTE>The demonstrative pronoun Łtasya$ in pāda c may refer to the guest:
              `he will obtain all his [i.e. the guest's] merits', hinting at some sort of karmic exchange.
                Nevertheless, I think that Łtasya$ points at the merits one can obtain by rituals listed 
                in the previous verse. This is suggested by passages such as the following:
                <sep/>
                Mahābhārata Supp. 13.14.379 ff.:<br/>
                   <skt>ahany ahani yo dadyāt kapilāṃ dvādaśīḥ samāḥi|<br/>
                        māsi māsi ca satreṇa yo yajeta sadā naraḥ|| <br/>
                        gavāṃ śatasahasraṃ ca yo dadyāj jyeṣṭhapuṣkare| <br/>
                        na taddharmaphalaṃ tulyam <b>atithir yasya tuṣyati</b>||</skt>
                <sep/>
                 Brahmavaivartapurāṇa 3.44--46:<br/>
                   <skt>atithiḥ pūjito yena pūjitāḥ sarvadevatāḥ|<br/>
                        <b>atithir yasya santuṣṭas</b> tasya tuṣṭo hariḥ svayam||<br/>
                        snānena sarvatīrtheṣu sarvadānena yat phalam|<br/>  
                        sarvavratopavāsena sarvayajñeṣu dīkṣayā|| <br/>
                        sarvais tapobhir vividhair nityair naimittikādibhiḥ|  <br/>
                        tad evātithisevāyāḥ kalāṃ nārhanti ṣoḍaśīm||</skt> 
        </NOTE>

<TEXT> †na gatim{ }atithijñasya gatim{ }āpnoti karhicit†|</TEXT>
        <APP>\va <LEM>na gatim{ }a°</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNb\msNc; na gati nā° \msNa, na tithim{ }a° \msCc\Ed</APP>
        <APP>\vb <LEM>karhicit</LEM> \msCa\Ed; karhacit \msCb\msCc\msNa\msNb\msNc</APP>

<TEXT> tasmād{ }atithim{ }āyāntam{ }abhigacchet{ }kṛtāñjaliḥ||</TEXT> 4.47
        <APP>\vc <LEM>°yānta°</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; °yānti° \msCc</APP>
        <PARAL>\vcd Ł= Vāyupurāṇa 2.17.8 = Brahmāṇḍapurāṇa 2.15.8; 
                        \kb Śivadharmottara 4.44ab: $
                        tasmād{ }atithim{ }āyāntam{ }anugacchet{ }kṛtāñjaliḥ</PARAL> 
        <!-- <TR>Therefore one should go up to the arriving guest with respectfully joined palms.</TR> -->

<TEXT> saktuprasthena caikena yajña āsīn{ }mahādbhutaḥ|</TEXT>
<APP>\va <LEM>saktu°</LEM> \eme; śanku° \msCa\msCb, śaṃktu° \msCc, śaktu° \msNa\msNc, śakthu° \msNb, śakti° \Ed</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>caikena</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\Ed; cekena \msNc</APP>
<APP>\vb <LEM>āsīn{ }mahādbhutaḥ</LEM> \corr; āsīn{ }mahadbhutaḥ \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb, āsī mahadbhutaḥ \msCc,                                               āsīt{ }mahādbhutaḥ \msNc, āsīn{ }mahadbhutam \Ed</APP>
        <!-- <TR>By one Łprastha$ of coarsely ground grains</TR> -->
        <NOTE>This verse is a reference to the story related by a mongoose in MBh 14.92--93: 
                A Brahmin who practises the vow of gleaning (Łuñcha$) and his family
                receive a guest. They feed the guest with the last morsels of the little food
                they have. In the end, the guest reveals that he is in fact Dharma (14.93.80cd) and as 
                a reward the family departs to heaven. The noble act of the poor Brahmin and his family
                is depicted as yielding greater rewards than Yudhiṣṭhira's grandiose horse-sacrifice. 
                (See some remarks on this story in Takahashi 2021.)</NOTE>

<TEXT> atithiprāptadānena svaśarīraṃ divaṃ gatam||</TEXT> 4.48
        <APP>\vc <LEM>°dānena</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; °prādānena \msCc</APP>
<APP>\vd <LEM>sva°</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb, <UNCL>sa°</UNCL> \msNc, sa° \Ed</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>°gatam</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; °gataḥ \msCc</APP>
        <!-- <TR>given to a guest, an extremely great sacrifice was performed [so to say],
        and his [the Brahmin's and his family members'] bodies (Łsvaśarīraṃ$) reached heaven.</TR> -->
         <NOTE><sep/>We would be forced to accept the reading of \Ed\ in pāda d if the expression
                were in the masculine (Łsaśarīro divaṃ gataḥ$). This would make sense
                and it would also echo expressions occuring e.g. in the Mahābhārata:
                3.164.33cd: <skt>paśya puṇyakṛtāṃ lokān saśarīro divaṃ vraja</skt>;
                14.5.10cd:  <skt>saṃjīvya kālam iṣṭaṃ ca saśarīro divaṃ gataḥ</skt>.
                It is tempting to emend the pāda accordingly, but I have retained 
                        Łsvaśarīraṃ divaṃ gatam$ and I interpret it as 
                        referring to the Brahmin's whole family (Łsva$).
                </NOTE>

<SUBCHAPTER>yameṣu damaḥ (5)</SUBCHAPTER>
<TRSUBCHAPTER>The fifth yama-rule: Self-restraint</TRSUBCHAPTER>

<TEXT> dama eva manuṣyāṇāṃ dharmasārasamuccayaḥ|</TEXT> 
        <APP>\vb <LEM>dharmasāra°</LEM> \eme; dharmaḥ sāra° \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\msNc, dharmabhāra° \Ed</APP>
        <PARAL>\vb Łcf.\ Mahābhārata Indeces 14.4.2477: $ śrotum icchāmi kārtsnyena dharmasārasamuccayam</PARAL>
        <!-- <TR>Self-restraint of humans is in itself the collected essence of Dharma.</TR> -->
% see MBh e.g. maybe quote some for support

<TEXT> damo dharmo damaḥ svargo damaḥ kīrtir{ }damaḥ sukham||</TEXT> 4.50
        <APP>\vc <LEM>svargo</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; svarga \msCc</APP>
        <APP>\vd <LEM>kīrtir{ }da°</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNb\Ed; kīrti da° \msCc\msNa\msNc</APP>
        <!-- <TR>Self-restraint is Dharma, Self-restraint is heaven, 
                 Self-restraint is fame, Self-restraint is happiness.</TR> -->

%                                              damaḥs \msCc
<TEXT> damo yajño damas{ }tīrthaṃ damaḥ puṇyaṃ damas{ }tapaḥ|</TEXT>
        <APP>\va <LEM>damas{ }tī°</LEM> \msCa\msCc\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; dama tī° \msCb</APP>
        <!-- <TR> Self-restraint is sacrifice, Self-restraint is a pilgrimage-place, 
                  Self-restraint is merit, Self-restraint is religious austerity.</TR> -->

% hīnam sandhi bridge?
<TEXT> damahīna{-}m{-}adharmaś{ }ca damaḥ kāmakulapradaḥ||</TEXT> 4.51
<APP>\vd <LEM>damaḥ</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc; dama \msCc, damaṃ \Ed</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>kāma°</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\Ed; kāmaṃ \msNc</APP>
        <!-- <TR>If one has no Self-restraint, there is no Dharma, 
                 [while] Self-restraint yields a multitude of desired objects.</TR> -->
% if everything else is there but no dama

% Flo kulakāmaprada?

<TEXT> nirdamaḥ kari mīnaś{ }ca pataṅgabhramaramṛgāḥ|</TEXT>
        <APP>\va <LEM>°damaḥ</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed; °dama \msCc</APP>
        <APP>\vb <LEM>°bhramara°</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\Ed\ \unmetr; °bhrama<UNCL>rā</UNCL>° \msNc</APP>
        <!-- <TR>The elephant, the fish, the moth, the bee and the deer are without Self-restraint.</TR> -->
        <NOTE>Note Łkari$ for Łkarī$ metri causa, and the end of pāda b (Ł°mṛgāḥ$), which 
                        should be treated metrically as if it read Ł°mrigāḥ$.</NOTE>
% nirdama is a rare word, but it's in the Harivaṃśa:
tava sapta vṛṣā gopa nirdamāḥ sasyaghātakāḥ HV_App.I,12.55

<TEXT> purūravo 'tilobhena atikāmena daṇḍakaḥ|</TEXT>
<APP>\va <LEM>purūravo</LEM> \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc; purorave \msCc, pururavā° \Ed</APP>
<APP>                 <LEM>tilobhena atikāmena</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\msNc; tikāmena atilobhena \Ed</APP>
        <APP>\vb <LEM>daṇḍakaḥ</LEM> \mssCaCbCc\msNa\msNb\msNc; puṇḍakaḥ \Ed</APP>
        <NOTE>Purūravas (double sandhi originally? purūravās ati° -- purūravā ati° -- purūravāti°).
        Pāda a may refer to the following passage in the Mahābhārata (1.70.16--18, 20ab):
        <br/>
                                 <skt>purūravās tato vidvān ilāyāṃ samapadyata|<br/>
                                 sā vai tasyābhavan mātā pitā ceti hi naḥ śrutam||<br/>
                                 trayodaśa samudrasya dvīpān aśnan purūravāḥ|<br/>
                                 amānuṣair vṛtaḥ sattvair mānuṣaḥ san mahāyaśāḥ||<br/>
                                 vipraiḥ sa vigrahaṃ cakre vīryonmattaḥ purūravāḥ|<br/>
                                 jahāra ca sa viprāṇāṃ ratnāny utkrośatām api||<br/>
                                        ... <br/>
                                 tato maharṣibhiḥ kruddhaiḥ śaptaḥ sadyo vyanaśyata|</skt>
        <sep/>
                        (``The wise Purūravas was born to Ilā. We heard that Ilā 
                                was both his mother and his father. 
                           The great Purūravas ruled over thirteen islands of the ocean
                           and, though human, he was always surrounded by superhuman beings.
                           Intoxicated with his power, Purūravas quarrelled with some Brahmins  
                           and robbed them of their wealth even though they were protesting. [...]
                           Therefore, cursed be the great Ṛṣis, he perished.'')
        <sep/>
        See also Buddhacarita 11.15 (Aiḍa = Purūravas):<sep/>
                <skt> aiḍaś ca rājā tridivaṃ vigāhya<br/> 
                        nītvāpi devīṃ vaśam urvaśīṃ tām|<br/>
                      lobhād ṛṣibhyaḥ kanakaṃ jihīrṣur  <br/>
                        jagāma nāśaṃ viṣayeṣv atṛptaḥ||</skt>
        <sep/>
                For Daṇḍa(ka)'s story, see Rāmāyaṇa 7.71.31 ff.:
                Daṇḍa meets Arajā, a beautiful girl, in a forest and rapes her. As a consequence, her father, Śukra/Bhārgava,
                destroyes Daṇḍa's kingdom, which thus becomes the desolate Daṇḍaka-forest.
                  </NOTE>
        <!-- <TR>Purūravas [perished] by excessive greed, Daṇḍaka by excessive desire,</TR> -->

<TEXT> sāgarāś{ }cātidarpeṇa atimānena rāvaṇaḥ||</TEXT> 4.56
        <APP>\va <LEM>sāgarā°</LEM> \eme; sagara° \msCa\msCb\msNa\msNb\msNc\Ed, sāgara° \msCc</APP>
        <!-- <TR>Sagara's sons by excessive pride, Rāvaṇa by excessive haughtiness,</TR> -->
        <NOTE><sep/>For two versions of the destruction of
                Sagara's sons, who were chasing the sacrificial horse of their father's Aśvamedha sacrifice,
                and by doing so disturbed Kapila's meditation, and who in turn burnt them to ashes,
                see Mahābhārata 3.105.9 ff. and Brahmāṇḍapurāṇa 2.52--53.
                <sep/>
                As for Rāvaṇa's haughtiness,
                especially the fact that he chose to be invincible by all creatures except humans,
                and its consequences,
                one should recall the story of the Rāmāyaṇa and Rāvaṇa's destruction brought about by Rāma therein.</NOTE>
gretil/mbh1_18uḥtmṭxt-03,105.024a             tataḥ pūrvottare deśe samudrasya mahīpate
gretil/mbh1_18uḥtmṭxt-03,105.024b*0522_01     vidāryamāṇāṃ pṛthivīṃ sāgarair baladarpitaiḥ
gretil/mbh1_18uḥtmṭxt:03,105.024c             vidārya pātālam atha saṃkruddhāḥ sagarātmajāḥ
gretil/mbh1_18uḥtmṭxt-03,105.024e             apaśyanta hayaṃ tatra vicarantaṃ mahītale
